<td colspan="2"></td>

<p><b>Edward Osborne Wilson</b> (born June 10, 1929), usually cited as <b>E. O. Wilson</b>, is an American biologist, researcher, theorist, naturalist and author. His biological specialty is <a href="/wiki/Myrmecology" title="Myrmecology">myrmecology</a>, the study of <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, on which he is the world's leading expert.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wilson has been called "the father of <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a>" and "the father of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-Father_of_Biodiversity_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Father_of_Biodiversity-4">[4]</a></sup> his <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmental</a> advocacy, and his <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular humanist">secular-humanist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deist" class="mw-redirect" title="Deist">deist</a> ideas pertaining to religious and ethical matters.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-5">[5]</a></sup> Among his greatest contributions to ecological theory is the theory of <a href="/wiki/Island_biogeography" class="mw-redirect" title="Island biogeography">island biogeography</a>, which he developed in collaboration with the mathematical ecologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_MacArthur" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert MacArthur">Robert MacArthur</a>, which was the foundation of the development of conservation area design, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Unified_neutral_theory_of_biodiversity" title="Unified neutral theory of biodiversity">unified neutral theory of biodiversity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hubbell" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen Hubbell">Stephen Hubbell</a>.</p>
<p>Wilson is (2014) the <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Pellegrino_University_Professor" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Pellegrino University Professor">Pellegrino University Research Professor, Emeritus</a> in Entomology for the Department of Organismic and Evolutionary Biology at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, a lecturer at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_Skeptical_Inquiry" title="Committee for Skeptical Inquiry">Committee for Skeptical Inquiry</a>. He is a Humanist Laureate of the <a href="/wiki/International_Academy_of_Humanism" title="International Academy of Humanism">International Academy of Humanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> He is a two-time winner of the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_General_Nonfiction" title="Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction">Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction</a> (for <i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/1979_Pulitzer_Prize" title="1979 Pulitzer Prize">1979</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ants" title="The Ants">The Ants</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/1991_Pulitzer_Prize" title="1991 Pulitzer Prize">1991</a>) and a <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> bestseller for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Conquest_of_Earth" title="The Social Conquest of Earth">The Social Conquest of Earth</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-CowlesG_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CowlesG-9">[9]</a></sup> <i>Letters to a Young Scientist</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-CowlesG_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CowlesG-9">[9]</a></sup> and <i>The Meaning of Human Existence</i>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wilson was born in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">Birmingham, Alabama</a>. According to his autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/Naturalist_(book)" title="Naturalist (book)">Naturalist</a></i>, he grew up mostly around <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> and in the countryside around <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile, Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> From an early age, he was interested in natural history. His parents, Edward and Inez Wilson, divorced when he was seven. The young naturalist grew up in several cities and towns, moving around with his father and his stepmother.</p>
<p>In the same year that his parents divorced, Wilson blinded himself in one eye in a fishing accident. He suffered for hours, but he continued fishing.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> He did not complain because he was anxious to stay outdoors. He did not seek medical treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> Several months later, his right pupil clouded over with a <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataract</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> He was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Pensacola_Hospital" title="Pensacola Hospital">Pensacola Hospital</a> to have the lens removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> Wilson writes, in his autobiography, that the "surgery was a terrifying [19th] century ordeal".<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> Wilson was left with full sight in his left eye, with a vision of 20/10.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> The 20/10 vision prompted him to focus on "little things": "I noticed butterflies and ants more than other kids did, and took an interest in them automatically."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although he had lost his <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic vision</a>, he could still see fine print and the hairs on the bodies of small insects.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> His reduced ability to observe mammals and birds led him to concentrate on <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.</p>
<p>At nine, Wilson undertook his first expeditions at the <a href="/wiki/Rock_Creek_Park" title="Rock Creek Park">Rock Creek Park</a> in Washington, DC. He began to collect insects and he gained a passion for butterflies. He would capture them using nets made with brooms, coat hangers, and cheesecloth bags.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> Going on these expeditions led to Wilson's fascination with ants. He describes in his autobiography how one day he pulled the bark of a rotting tree away and discovered <a href="/wiki/Acanthomyops" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthomyops">citronella ants</a> underneath.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> The worker ants he found were "short, fat, brilliant yellow, and emitted a strong lemony odor".<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> Wilson said the event left a "vivid and lasting impression on [him]".<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> He also earned the <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Scout_(Boy_Scouts_of_America)" title="Eagle Scout (Boy Scouts of America)">Eagle Scout</a> award and served as Nature Director of his <a href="/wiki/Boy_Scout" class="mw-redirect" title="Boy Scout">Boy Scout</a> summer camp. At the age of 18, intent on becoming an <a href="/wiki/Entomologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Entomologist">entomologist</a>, he began by collecting <a href="/wiki/Diptera" class="mw-redirect" title="Diptera">flies</a>, but the shortage of insect pins caused by World War II caused him to switch to <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, which could be stored in vials. With the encouragement of Marion R. Smith, a myrmecologist from the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a> in Washington, Wilson began a survey of all the ants of <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>. This study led him to report the first colony of <a href="/wiki/Fire_ants" class="mw-redirect" title="Fire ants">fire ants</a> in the US, near the port of Mobile.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Concerned that he might not be able to afford to go to a university, Wilson tried to enlist in the United States Army. He planned to earn U.S. government financial support for his education, but failed the Army medical examination due to his impaired eyesight.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Wilson was able to afford to enroll in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a> after all.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2015)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> There, he earned his B.S. and M.S. degrees in biology in 1950. In 1952 he transferred to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Society_of_Fellows" title="Harvard Society of Fellows">Harvard Society of Fellows</a>, he could travel on overseas expeditions, collecting ant species of Cuba and Mexico and travel the South Pacific, including Australia, New Guinea, Fiji, New Caledonia and Sri Lanka. In 1955, he received his Ph.D. and married Irene Kelley.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 1956 until 1996 Wilson was part of the faculty of Harvard. He began as an ant <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomist</a> and worked on understanding their evolution, how they developed into new species by escaping environmental disadvantages and moving into new habitats. He developed a theory of the "<a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a> cycle".<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>He collaborated with mathematician William Bossert, and discovered the chemical nature of ant communication, via <a href="/wiki/Pheromones" class="mw-redirect" title="Pheromones">pheromones</a>. In the 1960s he collaborated with mathematician and ecologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_MacArthur" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert MacArthur">Robert MacArthur</a>. Together, they tested the theory of species equilibrium on a tiny island in the Florida Keys. He eradicated all insect species and observed the re-population by new species. A book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Theory_of_Island_Biogeography" title="The Theory of Island Biogeography">The Theory of Island Biogeography</a></i> about this experiment became a standard ecology text.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1971, he published the book <i>The Insect Societies</i> about the biology of social insects like ants, bees, wasps and termites. In 1973, Wilson was appointed 'Curator of Insects' at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Comparative_Zoology" title="Museum of Comparative Zoology">Museum of Comparative Zoology</a>. In 1975, he published the book <i><a href="/wiki/Sociobiology:_The_New_Synthesis" title="Sociobiology: The New Synthesis">Sociobiology: The New Synthesis</a></i> applying his theories of insect behavior to vertebrates, and in the last chapter, humans. He speculated that evolved and inherited tendencies were responsible for hierarchical social organisation among humans. In 1978 he published <i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i>, which dealt with the role of biology in the evolution of human culture and won a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> for General Nonfiction.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1981 after collaborating with Charles Lumsden, he published <i>Genes, Mind and Culture</i>, a theory of <a href="/wiki/Gene-culture_coevolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Gene-culture coevolution">gene-culture coevolution</a>. In 1990 he published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ants" title="The Ants">The Ants</a></i>, co-written with <a href="/wiki/Bert_H%C3%B6lldobler" title="Bert Hölldobler">Bert Hölldobler</a>, his second Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1990s, he published <i>The Diversity of Life</i> (1992) an autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/Naturalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalist">Naturalist</a></i> (1994), and <i><a href="/wiki/Consilience_(book)" title="Consilience (book)">Consilience: The Unity of Knowledge</a></i> (1998) about the unity of the natural and social sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1996, Wilson officially retired from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, where he continues to hold the positions of <a href="/wiki/Emeritus" title="Emeritus">Professor Emeritus</a> and Honorary Curator in Entomology. He founded the E.O. Wilson Biodiversity Foundation, which finances the <a href="/wiki/PEN/E._O._Wilson_Literary_Science_Writing_Award" title="PEN/E. O. Wilson Literary Science Writing Award">PEN/E. O. Wilson Literary Science Writing Award</a> and is an "independent foundation" at the <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_School_of_the_Environment" title="Nicholas School of the Environment">Nicholas School of the Environment</a>, Duke University. Wilson became a special lecturer at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> as part of the agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-Tenure_at_Duke_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tenure_at_Duke-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wilson has published 14 books during the new millennium: <i>The Future of Life</i>, 2002, <i>Pheidole in the New World: A Dominant, Hyperdiverse Ant Genus,</i> 2003, <i>From So Simple a Beginning: Darwin's Four Great Books,</i> 2005, <i>The Creation: An Appeal to Save Life on Earth,</i> September 2006, <i>Nature Revealed: Selected Writings</i> 1949–2006, <i>The Superorganism: The Beauty, Elegance, and Strangeness of Insect Societies,</i> 2009, <i><a href="/wiki/Anthill:_A_Novel" title="Anthill: A Novel">Anthill: A Novel</a></i> April 2010, <i>Kingdom of Ants: Jose Celestino Mutis and the Dawn of Natural History in the New World</i>, 2010, <i>The Leafcutter Ants: Civilization by Instinct,</i> 2011, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Conquest_of_Earth" title="The Social Conquest of Earth">The Social Conquest of Earth</a></i>, 2012, <i>Half-Earth: Our Planet's Fight for Life</i>, 2016.</p>
<p>He published 3 books in 2014 alone: <i>Letters to a Young Scientist</i>, <i>A Window on Eternity: A Biologist's Walk Through Gorongosa National Park,</i> and <i>The Meaning of Human Existence</i>.</p>
<p>He and his wife Irene reside in <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Massachusetts" title="Lexington, Massachusetts">Lexington, Massachusetts</a>. His daughter, Catherine, and her husband Jonathan, reside in nearby <a href="/wiki/Stow,_Massachusetts" title="Stow, Massachusetts">Stow, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work">Work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Sociobiology:_The_New_Synthesis,_1975"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociobiology:_The_New_Synthesis.2C_1975"><i>Sociobiology: The New Synthesis</i>, 1975</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sociobiology: The New Synthesis, 1975">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wilson used sociobiology and evolutionary principles to explain the behavior of social insects and then to understand the social behavior of other animals, including humans, thus established sociobiology as a new scientific field. He argued that all animal behavior, including that of humans, is the product of <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, environmental stimuli, and past experiences, and that <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> is an illusion. He has referred to the biological basis of behaviour as the "genetic leash".<sup id="cite_ref-con_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-con-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:127–128</sup> The sociobiological view is that all animal social behavior is governed by <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> rules worked out by the laws of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>. This theory and research proved to be seminal, controversial, and influential.<sup id="cite_ref-soul_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soul-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:210ff</sup></p>
<p>Wilson has argued that the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_selection" title="Unit of selection">unit of selection</a> is a gene, the basic element of heredity. The <i>target</i> of selection is normally the individual who carries an ensemble of genes of certain kinds. With regard to the use of <a href="/wiki/Kin_selection" title="Kin selection">kin selection</a> in explaining the behavior of <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial</a> <a href="/wiki/Insects" class="mw-redirect" title="Insects">insects</a>, the "new view that I'm proposing is that it was <a href="/wiki/Group_selection" title="Group selection">group selection</a> all along, an idea first roughly formulated by Darwin."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sociobiological research is particularly controversial with regard to its application to humans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The theory established a scientific argument for rejecting the common doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a>, which holds that human beings are born without any innate mental content and that culture functions to increase human knowledge and aid in survival and success.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>In the final chapter of the book <i>Sociobiology</i> and in the full text of his <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>-winning <i>On Human Nature</i>, Wilson argues, that the human mind is shaped as much by genetic inheritance as it is by culture if not more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There are limits on just how much influence social and environmental factors can have in altering human behavior.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>Sociobiology</i> was initially met with substantial criticism. Several of Wilson's colleagues at Harvard,<sup id="cite_ref-grafen75_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grafen75-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lewontin" title="Richard Lewontin">Richard Lewontin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a>, were strongly opposed to his ideas regarding sociobiology. Gould, Lewontin, and others from the <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology_Study_Group" title="Sociobiology Study Group">Sociobiology Study Group</a> from the Boston area wrote "Against 'Sociobiology'" in an <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a> criticizing Wilson's "deterministic view of human society and human action".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Although attributed to members of the Sociobiology Study Group, it seems that Lewontin was the main author.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_O._Wilson_2006_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_O._Wilson_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> In a 2011 interview, Wilson said, "I believe Gould was a charlatan. I believe that he was ... seeking reputation and credibility as a scientist and writer, and he did it consistently by distorting what other scientists were saying and devising arguments based upon that distortion."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marshall_Sahlins" title="Marshall Sahlins">Marshall Sahlins</a>'s 1976 work <i>The Use and Abuse of Biology</i> was a direct criticism of Wilson's theories.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was also political opposition. Sociobiology re-ignited the <a href="/wiki/Nature_and_nurture" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature and nurture">nature and nurture</a> debate. Wilson was accused of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">misogyny</a>, and sympathy to <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In one incident in November 1978, his lecture was attacked by the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_Against_Racism" title="International Committee Against Racism">International Committee Against Racism</a>, a front group of the Marxist <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Labor_Party_(United_States)" title="Progressive Labor Party (United States)">Progressive Labor Party</a>, where one member poured a pitcher of water on Wilson's head and chanted "Wilson, you're all wet" at an <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">AAAS</a> conference.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Wilson later spoke of the incident as a source of pride: "I believe...I was the only scientist in modern times to be physically attacked for an idea."<sup id="cite_ref-Nova_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nova-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Objections from evangelical Christians included those of Paul E. Rothrock in 1987: "... sociobiology has the potential of becoming a religion of scientific materialism."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Mary_Midgley" title="Mary Midgley">Mary Midgley</a> encountered <i>Sociobiology</i> in the process of writing <i>Beast and Man</i> (1996) <sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and significantly rewrote the book to offer a critique of Wilson's views. Midgley praised the book for the study of animal behavior, clarity, scholarship, and encyclopedic scope, but extensively critiqued Wilson for conceptual confusion, scientism, and anthropomorphism of genetics.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Michael McGoodwin paraphrased and quoted Wilson (pp.&#160;16 and 222) on <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-mcgoodwin.net_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgoodwin.net-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (October 2015)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup> "Sociobiology is defined as the scientific or systematic study of the biological basis of all forms of social behavior, in all kinds of organisms including man, and incorporating knowledge from <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, in order to derive general principles concerning the biological properties of entire societies. "If humankind evolved by Darwinian natural selection, [then] genetic chance and environmental necessity, not God, made the species." "The brain [and the mind] exists because it promotes the survival and multiplication of the genes that direct its assembly." The two apparent dilemmas we face therefore are: (1) We lack any goal external to our biological nature (for even religions evolve to enhance the persistence and influence of their practitioners). Will the transcendental goals of societies dissolve, and will our post-ideological societies regress steadily toward self-indulgence? (2) Morality evolved as instinct. "Which of the censors and motivators should be obeyed and which ones might better be curtailed or sublimated?" Although much human diversity in behavior is culturally influenced, some has been shown to be genetic&#160;– rapid acquisition of language, human unpredictability, hypertrophied social institutions, altruism and religions. "Religious practices that consistently enhance survival and procreation of the practitioners will propagate the physiological controls that favor the acquisition of the practices during single lifetimes." Unthinking submission to the communal will promotes the fitness of the members of the tribe. Even submission to secular religions and cults involve willing subordination of the individual to the group. Religious practices confer biological advantages."<sup id="cite_ref-mcgoodwin.net_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgoodwin.net-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (October 2015)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span id="On_Human_Nature,_1978"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Human_Nature.2C_1978"><i>On Human Nature</i>, 1978</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: On Human Nature, 1978">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wilson wrote in his 1978 book <i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i>, "The evolutionary epic is probably the best myth we will ever have." Wilson's use of the word "myth" provides people with meaningful placement in time celebrating shared heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Wilson's fame prompted use of the morphed phrase <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_evolution" title="Epic of evolution">epic of evolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1999, he explained its need:<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Human beings must have an epic, a sublime account of how the world was created and how humanity became part of it... Religious epics satisfy another primal need. They confirm we are part of something greater than ourselves... The way to achieve our epic that unites human spirituality, instead of cleave it, is to compose it from the best empirical knowledge that science and history can provide.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>He said that "[t]he true evolutionary epic retold as poetry, is as intrinsically ennobling as any religious epic."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> He pointed to several scientists who had contributed to building this epic, particularly <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ardrey" title="Robert Ardrey">Robert Ardrey</a> who "continues as the lyric poet of human evolution, capturing the Homeric quality of the subject that so many scientists by and large feel, but are unable to put into words."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (October 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Naturalistic and liberal religious writers have picked up on the term epic of evolution and used it in a number of texts or used other terms to refer to the idea: <i>Universe Story</i> (<a href="/wiki/Brian_Swimme" title="Brian Swimme">Brian Swimme</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Haught" title="John F. Haught">John F. Haught</a>), <i>Great Story</i> (Connie Barlow, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dowd" title="Michael Dowd">Michael Dowd</a>), <i>Everybody's Story</i> (<a href="/wiki/Loyal_Rue" title="Loyal Rue">Loyal Rue</a><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>), <i>New Story</i> (<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Berry" title="Thomas Berry">Thomas Berry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, Brian Swimme) and <i>Cosmic Evolution</i> (<a href="/wiki/Eric_Chaisson" title="Eric Chaisson">Eric Chaisson</a><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">Cosmologist</a> Brian Swimme said in a 1997 interview:<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> "I think that what E. O. Wilson is trying to suggest is that to be fully human, a person has to see that life has a heroic dimension... I think for the scientist, and for other people, it's a question of, "Is the universe valuable? Is it sacred? Is it holy? Or is the human agenda all that matters?" I just don't think we're that stupid to continue in a way that continues to destroy. I'm hopeful that the Epic of Evolution will be yet another strategy in our culture that will lead our consciousness out of a very tight, human-centered materialism."</p>
<h3><span id="The_Ants,_1990"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Ants.2C_1990"><i>The Ants</i>, 1990</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Ants, 1990">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wilson, along with <a href="/wiki/Bert_H%C3%B6lldobler" title="Bert Hölldobler">Bert Hölldobler</a>, carried out a systematic study of ants and ant behavior,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> culminating in the 1990 encyclopedic work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ants" title="The Ants">The Ants</a></i>. Because much self-sacrificing behavior on the part of individual ants can be explained on the basis of their genetic interests in the survival of the sisters, with whom they share 75% of their genes (though the actual case is some species' queens mate with multiple males and therefore some workers in a colony would only be 25% related), Wilson argued for a sociobiological explanation for all social behavior on the model of the behavior of the social insects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In his more recent work,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2015)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> he has sought to defend his views against the criticism of younger scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Deborah_Gordon" class="mw-redirect" title="Deborah Gordon">Deborah Gordon</a>, whose results challenge the idea that ant behavior is as rigidly predictable as Wilson's explanations make it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Wilson has said in reference to ants "<a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> was right, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> works, it is just that he had the wrong species".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> He meant that while ants and other <a href="/wiki/Eusocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusocial">eusocial</a> species appear to live in <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communist</a>-like societies, they only do so because they are forced to do so from their basic biology, as they lack reproductive independence: worker ants, being sterile, need their ant-queen in order to survive as a colony and a species, and individual ants cannot reproduce without a queen and are thus forced to live in centralised societies. Humans, however, do possess reproductive independence so they can give birth to offspring without the need of a "queen", and in fact humans enjoy their maximum level of Darwinian fitness only when they look after themselves and their offspring, while finding innovative ways to use the societies they live in for their own benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Consilience,_1998"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Consilience.2C_1998"><i>Consilience</i>, 1998</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Consilience, 1998">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his 1998 book <i><a href="/wiki/Consilience_(book)" title="Consilience (book)">Consilience: The Unity of Knowledge</a></i>, Wilson discussed methods that have been used to unite the sciences, and might be able to unite the sciences with the humanities. Wilson used the term "<a href="/wiki/Consilience" title="Consilience">consilience</a>" to describe the synthesis of knowledge from different specialized fields of human endeavor.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He defined <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> as a collection of <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> rules, the genetic patterns of mental development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He argued that culture and rituals are products, not parts, of human nature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He said <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a> is not part of human nature, but our appreciation of art is.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He suggested that concepts such as art appreciation, fear of snakes, or the <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a> <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a> (<a href="/wiki/Westermarck_effect" title="Westermarck effect">Westermarck effect</a>) could be studied by scientific methods of the natural sciences and be part of interdisciplinary research.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Previously,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2015)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> these phenomena were only part of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociological</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a> studies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_and_political_beliefs">Spiritual and political beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spiritual and political beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_humanism">Scientific humanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scientific humanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wilson coined the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_humanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific humanism">scientific humanism</a></i> as "the only <a href="/wiki/Worldview" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldview">worldview</a> compatible with science's growing knowledge of the real world and the laws of nature".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (October 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Wilson argued that it is best suited to improve the human condition. In 2003, he was one of the signers of the <a href="/wiki/Humanism_and_Its_Aspirations" title="Humanism and Its Aspirations"><i>Humanist Manifesto</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="God_and_religion">God and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: God and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> interview published on 21 January 2015, Wilson said that "Religion 'is dragging us down' and must be eliminated 'for the sake of human progress<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>", and "So I would say that for the sake of human progress, the best thing we could possibly do would be to diminish, to the point of eliminating, religious faiths."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> On the question of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, Wilson has described his position as <i>provisional deism</i><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and explicitly denied the label of "<a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a>", preferring "<a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostic</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-slate_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slate-47">[47]</a></sup> He has explained his faith as a trajectory away from traditional beliefs: "I drifted away from the church, not definitively agnostic or atheistic, just <a href="/wiki/Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist">Baptist</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> no more."<sup id="cite_ref-con_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-con-15">[15]</a></sup> Wilson argues that the belief in God and rituals of religion are products of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">evolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> He argues that they should not be rejected or dismissed, but further investigated by science to better understand their significance to human nature. In his book <i>The Creation</i>, Wilson suggests that scientists ought to "offer the hand of friendship" to religious leaders and build an alliance with them, stating that "Science and religion are two of the most potent forces on Earth and they should come together to save the creation."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wilson made an appeal to the religious community on the lecture circuit at Midland College, Texas, for example, and that "the appeal received a 'massive reply'", that a covenant had been written and that a "partnership will work to a substantial degree as time goes on".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wilson has said that, if he could start his life over he would work in <a href="/wiki/Microbial_ecology" title="Microbial ecology">microbial ecology</a>, when discussing the reinvigoration of his original fields of study since the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> He studied the <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a> of the 20th century and their relationship to modern society, and in 1998 argued for an <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> approach at the Capitol:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Now when you cut a forest, an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient forest">ancient forest</a> in particular, you are not just removing a lot of big <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a> and a few <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> fluttering around in the <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(forest)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canopy (forest)">canopy</a>. You are drastically imperiling a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> within a few square miles of you. The number of these species may go to tens of thousands. ... Many of them are still unknown to science, and science has not yet discovered the key role undoubtedly played in the maintenance of that <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>, as in the case of <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, and many of the <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-slide2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slide2-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Wilson has been part of the international <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation movement</a>, as a consultant to Columbia University's <a href="/wiki/Earth_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth Institute">Earth Institute</a>, as a director of the American Museum of Natural History, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_International" title="Conservation International">Conservation International</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Nature_Conservancy" title="The Nature Conservancy">The Nature Conservancy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Academy_of_Achievement_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academy_of_Achievement-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Understanding the scale of the extinction crisis has led him to advocate for forest protection,<sup id="cite_ref-slide2_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slide2-52">[52]</a></sup> including the "Act to Save America's Forests", first introduced in 1998, until 2008, but never passed.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Forests_Now_Declaration" title="Forests Now Declaration">Forests Now Declaration</a> calls for new markets-based mechanisms to protect tropical forests.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2014, Wilson called for setting aside 50% of the earth's surface for other species to thrive in as the only possible strategy to solve the extinction crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Wilson's scientific and <a href="/wiki/Conservationism" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservationism">conservation</a> honors include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Member, <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>, 1969</li>
<li>U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, 1976</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Leidy_Award" title="Leidy Award">Leidy Award</a>, 1979, from the Academy of Natural Sciences of Philadelphia<sup id="cite_ref-PANSP_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PANSP-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_General_Non-Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulitzer Prize for General Non-Fiction">Pulitzer Prize</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/1979_Pulitzer_Prize" title="1979 Pulitzer Prize">1979</a> <sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tyler_Prize_for_Environmental_Achievement" title="Tyler Prize for Environmental Achievement">Tyler Prize for Environmental Achievement</a>, 1984</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/ECI_Prize" title="ECI Prize">ECI Prize</a>, International Ecology Institute, terrestrial ecology, 1987</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">Honorary doctorate</a> from the Faculty of Mathematics and Science at <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University" title="Uppsala University">Uppsala University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, 1987<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Achievement" title="Academy of Achievement">Academy of Achievement Golden Plate Award</a>, 1988</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crafoord_Prize" title="Crafoord Prize">Crafoord Prize</a>, 1990, a prize awarded by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a> <sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_General_Non-Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulitzer Prize for General Non-Fiction">Pulitzer Prize</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ants" title="The Ants">The Ants</a></i> (with <a href="/wiki/Bert_H%C3%B6lldobler" title="Bert Hölldobler">Bert Hölldobler</a>), 1991</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Prize_for_Biology" title="International Prize for Biology">International Prize for Biology</a>, 1993</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Dauphin_Island_Sea_Lab" title="Dauphin Island Sea Lab">Dauphin Island Sea Lab</a> christened its newest research vessel the <i>R/V E.O. Wilson</i> in 2005.</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_works">Main works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=E._O._Wilson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Main works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation journal">"Character displacement". <i>Systematic Zoology</i>. <b>5</b> (2): 49–64. 1956. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/2411924">2411924</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.2307%2F2411924">10.2307/2411924</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AE.+O.+Wilson&amp;rft.atitle=Character+displacement&amp;rft.date=1956&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Systematic+Zoology&amp;rft.pages=49-64&amp;rft.volume=5&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F2411924&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2411924&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, coauthored with William Brown Jr.; paper honored in 1986 as a Science Citation Classic, i.e., as one of the most frequently cited scientific papers of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>

MacArthur</a></li>
<li><i>The Insect Societies</i>, 1971, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">Harvard University Press</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-674-45490-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-674-45490-1">0-674-45490-1</a></li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i>, 1979, Harvard University Press, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-674-01638-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-674-01638-6">0-674-01638-6</a>, winner of the 1979 Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction.</li>

<div id="Pulitzer_Prize_for_General_Nonfiction_.281976.E2.80.932000.29" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_General_Nonfiction" title="Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction">Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction</a> (1976–2000)</div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Why_Survive%3F_Being_Old_in_America" title="Why Survive? Being Old in America">Why Survive? Being Old in America</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Neil_Butler" title="Robert Neil Butler">Robert Neil Butler</a> (1976)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Beautiful_Swimmers" title="Beautiful Swimmers">Beautiful Swimmers</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_W._Warner" title="William W. Warner">William W. Warner</a> (1977)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Dragons_of_Eden" title="The Dragons of Eden">The Dragons of Eden</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> (1978)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Human_Nature" title="On Human Nature">On Human Nature</a></i> by <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Edward O. Wilson</a> (1979)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del,_Escher,_Bach" title="Gödel, Escher, Bach">Gödel, Escher, Bach: an Eternal Golden Braid</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hofstadter" title="Douglas Hofstadter">Douglas Hofstadter</a> (1980)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Fin-de-si%C3%A8cle_Vienna" title="Fin-de-siècle Vienna">Fin-de-siècle Vienna</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Emil_Schorske" title="Carl Emil Schorske">Carl E. Schorske</a> (1981)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Soul_of_a_New_Machine" title="The Soul of a New Machine">The Soul of a New Machine</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Tracy_Kidder" title="Tracy Kidder">Tracy Kidder</a> (1982)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Is_There_No_Place_on_Earth_for_Me%3F" title="Is There No Place on Earth for Me?">Is There No Place on Earth for Me?</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Susan_Sheehan" title="Susan Sheehan">Susan Sheehan</a> (1983)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Transformation_of_American_Medicine" title="The Social Transformation of American Medicine">The Social Transformation of American Medicine</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Starr" title="Paul Starr">Paul Starr</a> (1984)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Good_War" title="The Good War">The Good War</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Studs_Terkel" title="Studs Terkel">Studs Terkel</a> (1985)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Common_Ground_(book)" title="Common Ground (book)">Common Ground: A Turbulent Decade in the Lives of Three American Families</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/J._Anthony_Lukas" title="J. Anthony Lukas">J. Anthony Lukas</a> / <i><a href="/wiki/Move_Your_Shadow" title="Move Your Shadow">Move Your Shadow</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lelyveld" title="Joseph Lelyveld">Joseph Lelyveld</a> (1986)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Arab_and_Jew:_Wounded_Spirits_in_a_Promised_Land" title="Arab and Jew: Wounded Spirits in a Promised Land">Arab and Jew: Wounded Spirits in a Promised Land</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/David_K._Shipler" title="David K. Shipler">David K. Shipler</a> (1987)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_the_Atomic_Bomb" title="The Making of the Atomic Bomb">The Making of the Atomic Bomb</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rhodes" title="Richard Rhodes">Richard Rhodes</a> (1988)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/A_Bright_Shining_Lie" title="A Bright Shining Lie">A Bright Shining Lie</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Sheehan" title="Neil Sheehan">Neil Sheehan</a> (1989)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/And_Their_Children_After_Them" title="And Their Children After Them">And Their Children After Them</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Dale_Maharidge" title="Dale Maharidge">Dale Maharidge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Williamson_(photographer)" title="Michael Williamson (photographer)">Michael Williamson</a> (1990)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Ants" title="The Ants">The Ants</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Bert_H%C3%B6lldobler" title="Bert Hölldobler">Bert Hölldobler</a> and <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Edward O. Wilson</a> (1991)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Prize:_The_Epic_Quest_for_Oil,_Money,_and_Power" title="The Prize: The Epic Quest for Oil, Money, and Power">The Prize: The Epic Quest for Oil, Money, and Power</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Yergin" title="Daniel Yergin">Daniel Yergin</a> (1992)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Lincoln_at_Gettysburg" title="Lincoln at Gettysburg">Lincoln at Gettysburg: The Words That Remade America</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Garry_Wills" title="Garry Wills">Garry Wills</a> (1993)</li>
